<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Yuri (genre)</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shiroi00_cover1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="300" height="357" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5a/Shiroi00_cover1.jpg/230px-Shiroi00_cover1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5a/Shiroi00_cover1.jpg 1.5x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shiroi00_cover1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dealing with the romance between an "average blonde" and a "stately brunette" at a girls' boarding school, <i><a href="/wiki/Shiroi_Heya_no_Futari" title="Shiroi Heya no Futari">Shiroi Heya no Futari</a></i>, the first <i>yuri</i> manga,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aft-yuri1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-aft-yuri1-1">[1]</a></sup> established archetypes that can be seen even in <i>yuri</i> works of the 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shiro-oka1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-shiro-oka1-2">[2]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><b>Yuri</b></i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">百合</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, "<a href="/wiki/Lilium" title="Lilium">lily</a>")</span>, also known by the <a href="/wiki/Wasei-eigo" title="Wasei-eigo">wasei-eigo</a> construction <b>Girls' Love</b> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">ガールズラブ</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>gāruzu rabu</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hime-1x_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-hime-1x-3">[3]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> jargon term for content and a genre involving love between women in <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a>, and related Japanese media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alc-yurix_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-alc-yurix-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyzo_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-cyzo-5">[5]</a></sup> <i>Yuri</i> focuses on the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Romantic_orientation" title="Romantic orientation">romantic orientation</a> aspects of the relationship, or both, the latter of which sometimes being called <i>shōjo-ai</i> by Western fandom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whatisyuri_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-whatisyuri-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The themes <i>yuri</i> deals with have their roots in the Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lesbian_fiction" title="Lesbian fiction">lesbian fiction</a> of the early twentieth century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basho-1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-basho-1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about-2_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-about-2-8">[8]</a></sup> with pieces such as <i>Yaneura no Nishojo</i> by <a href="/wiki/Nobuko_Yoshiya" title="Nobuko Yoshiya">Nobuko Yoshiya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aas-yuri_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-aas-yuri-9">[9]</a></sup> Nevertheless, it is not until the 1970s that lesbian-themed works began to appear in manga, by the hand of artists such as <a href="/wiki/Ryoko_Yamagishi" title="Ryoko Yamagishi">Ryoko Yamagishi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Riyoko_Ikeda" title="Riyoko Ikeda">Riyoko Ikeda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aft-yuri1_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-aft-yuri1-1">[1]</a></sup> The 1990s brought new trends in manga and anime, as well as in <a href="/wiki/D%C5%8Djinshi" title="Dōjinshi">dōjinshi</a> productions, along with more acceptance for this kind of content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuri-esu_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-yuri-esu-10">[10]</a></sup> In 2003, the first manga magazine specifically dedicated to <i>yuri</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Yuri_Shimai" title="Yuri Shimai">Yuri Shimai</a></i>, was launched, and this was followed by its revival <i><a href="/wiki/Comic_Yuri_Hime" title="Comic Yuri Hime">Comic Yuri Hime</a></i>, which was launched after the former was discontinued in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pedia-yuri_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-pedia-yuri-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pedia-yuri1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-pedia-yuri1-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although <i>yuri</i> originated in female-targeted (<a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Djo_manga" title="Shōjo manga">shōjo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Josei_manga" title="Josei manga">josei</a>) works, today it is featured in male-targeted (<a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dnen_manga" title="Shōnen manga">shōnen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seinen_manga" title="Seinen manga">seinen</a>) ones as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about-2_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-about-2-8">[8]</a></sup> <i>Yuri</i> manga from male-targeted magazines include titles such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kannazuki_no_Miko" title="Kannazuki no Miko">Kannazuki no Miko</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Strawberry_Panic!" title="Strawberry Panic!">Strawberry Panic!</a></i>, as well as those from <i>Comic Yuri Hime'</i>s male-targeted sister magazine, <i><a href="/wiki/Comic_Yuri_Hime_S" title="Comic Yuri Hime S">Comic Yuri Hime S</a></i>, which was launched in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ichi-yuri1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ichi-yuri1-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Anime_and_manga" title="Template:Anime and manga"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Anime_and_manga" title="Template talk:Anime and manga"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anime_and_manga&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<p>The word <i>yuri</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">百合</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> literally means "<a href="/wiki/Lilium" title="Lilium">lily</a>", and is a relatively common Japanese feminine name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alc-yurix_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-alc-yurix-4">[4]</a></sup> In 1976, Bungaku Itō, editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Barazoku" title="Barazoku">Barazoku</a></i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">薔薇族</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, lit. rose tribe)</span>, a magazine geared primarily towards gay men, first used the term <i>yurizoku</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">百合族</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, lit. lily tribe)</span> in reference to female readers in the title of a column of letters called <i>Yurizoku no heya</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">百合族の部屋</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, lit. lily tribe's room)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heya-1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-heya-1-14">[14]</a></sup> It is unclear whether this was the first instance of this usage of the term. Not all women whose letters appeared in this short-lived column were necessarily lesbians, but some were and gradually an association developed. For example, the <i><a href="/wiki/Yaoi" title="Yaoi">tanbi</a></i> magazine <i>Allan</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">アラン</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Aran</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> began running a <i>Yuri Tsūshin</i> <span style="font-weight:normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">百合通信</span><span class="t_nihongo_help"><sup><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span style="color:#00e; font:bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration:none; padding:0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup></span>, "Lily Communication")</span> personal ad column in July 1983 for "lesbiennes" to communicate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Along the way, many <a href="/wiki/D%C5%8Djinshi" title="Dōjinshi">dōjinshi</a> circles incorporated the name "Yuri" or "Yuriko" into lesbian-themed <a href="/wiki/Hentai" title="Hentai">hentai</a> (pornographic) dōjinshi, and the "<a href="/wiki/Zoku" title="Zoku">zoku</a>" or "tribe" portion of this word was subsequently dropped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whatisyuri_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-whatisyuri-6">[6]</a></sup> Since then, the meaning has drifted from its mostly pornographic connotation to describe the portrayal of intimate love, sex, or the intimate emotional connections between women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about-1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-about-1-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_vs._Western_usage">Japanese vs. Western usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yuri_(genre)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Japanese vs. Western usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2009, the term <i>yuri</i> is used in Japan to mean the depiction of attraction between women (whether sexual or romantic; explicit or implied) in manga, anime, and related entertainment media, as well as the genre of stories primarily dealing with this content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyzo_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-cyzo-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about-1_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-about-1-16">[16]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wasei-eigo" title="Wasei-eigo">wasei-eigo</a> construction "Girls Love" <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">ガールズラブ</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>gāruzu rabu</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span>, occasionally spelled "Girl's Love" or "Girls' Love", or abbreviated as "GL", is also used with this meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hime-1x_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-hime-1x-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about-1_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-about-1-16">[16]</a></sup> <i>Yuri</i> is generally a form of <a href="/wiki/Fanspeak" title="Fanspeak">fanspeak</a> amongst fans, but its usage by authors and publishers has increased since 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hime-1x_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-hime-1x-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyzo_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-cyzo-5">[5]</a></sup> The term "Girls Love", on the other hand, is primarily used by the publishers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about-1_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-about-1-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl-yuri_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-gl-yuri-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In North America, <i>yuri</i> has initially been used to denote only the most explicit end of the spectrum, deemed primarily as a variety of hentai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whatisyuri_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-whatisyuri-6">[6]</a></sup> Following the pattern of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dnen-ai" title="Shōnen-ai">shōnen-ai</a></i>, a term already in use in North America to describe content involving non-sexual relationships between men, Western fans coined the term <i>shōjo-ai</i> to describe <i>yuri</i> without explicit sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whatisyuri_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-whatisyuri-6">[6]</a></sup> In Japan, the term <i>shōjo-ai</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">少女愛</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, lit. girl love)</span> is not used with this meaning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whatisyuri_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-whatisyuri-6">[6]</a></sup> and instead tends to denote <a href="/wiki/Pedophilia" title="Pedophilia">pedophilia</a> (actual or perceived), with a similar meaning to the term <i><a href="/wiki/Lolicon" title="Lolicon">lolicon</a></i> (Lolita complex).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl-1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-gl-1-18">[18]</a></sup> The Western use of <i>yuri</i> has broadened in the 2000s, picking up connotations from the Japanese use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about-1_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-about-1-16">[16]</a></sup> American publishing companies such as <a href="/wiki/Yuricon" title="Yuricon">ALC Publishing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seven_Seas_Entertainment" title="Seven Seas Entertainment">Seven Seas Entertainment</a> have also adopted the Japanese usage of the term to classify their <i>yuri</i> manga publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alc-yuri1_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-alc-yuri1-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven-seas_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-seven-seas-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thematic_history">Thematic history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yuri_(genre)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Thematic history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Among the first Japanese authors to produce works about love between women was <a href="/wiki/Nobuko_Yoshiya" title="Nobuko Yoshiya">Nobuko Yoshiya</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aas-yuri_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-aas-yuri-9">[9]</a></sup> a novelist active in the <a href="/wiki/Taish%C5%8D_period" title="Taishō period">Taishō</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_period" title="Shōwa period">Shōwa</a> periods of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoshiya_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-yoshiya-21">[21]</a></sup> Yoshiya was a pioneer in <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Lesbian_literature" title="Lesbian literature">lesbian literature</a>, including the early twentieth century <a href="/wiki/Class_S_(genre)" title="Class S (genre)">Class S genre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-androgyny_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-androgyny-22">[22]</a></sup> These kinds of stories depict lesbian attachments as emotionally intense yet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonic_relationship" title="Platonic relationship">platonic relationships</a>, destined to be curtailed by graduation from school, marriage, or death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoshiya_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-yoshiya-21">[21]</a></sup> The root of this genre is in part the contemporary belief that same-sex love was a transitory and normal part of female development leading into <a href="/wiki/Heterosexuality" title="Heterosexuality">heterosexuality</a> and motherhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flower_Tales_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flower_Tales-23">[23]</a></sup> Class S stories in particular tell of strong emotional bonds between schoolgirls, a mutual <a href="/wiki/Limerence" title="Limerence">crush</a> between an <a href="/wiki/Senpai_and_k%C5%8Dhai" title="Senpai and kōhai">upperclassman and an underclassman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-androgyny_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-androgyny-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around the 1970s, <i>yuri</i> began to appear in <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Djo" title="Shōjo">shōjo</a> manga,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aft-yuri1_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-aft-yuri1-1">[1]</a></sup> presenting some of the characteristics found in the lesbian literature of the early twentieth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basho-1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-basho-1-7">[7]</a></sup> This early <i>yuri</i> generally features an older looking, more sophisticated woman, and a younger, more awkward admirer. The two deal with some sort of unfortunate schism between their families, and when rumors of their lesbian relationship spread, they are received as a scandal. The outcome is a tragedy, with the more sophisticated girl somehow dying at the end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basho-1_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-basho-1-7">[7]</a></sup> In general, the <i>yuri</i> manga of this time could not avoid a tragic ending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howtoread_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-howtoread-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dream-1_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-dream-1-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ryoko_Yamagishi" title="Ryoko Yamagishi">Ryoko Yamagishi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Shiroi_Heya_no_Futari" title="Shiroi Heya no Futari">Shiroi Heya no Futari</a></i>, the first manga involving a lesbian relationship,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aft-yuri1_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-aft-yuri1-1">[1]</a></sup> is a prime example, as it was "prototypical" for many yuri stories of the 1970s and 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canada1_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-canada1-26">[26]</a></sup> It is also in the 1970s that shōjo manga began to deal with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transsexualism" title="Transsexualism">transsexualism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transvestism" title="Transvestism">transvestism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuri-sex1_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-yuri-sex1-27">[27]</a></sup> sometimes depicting female characters as manly looking, which was inspired by the women playing male roles in the <a href="/wiki/Takarazuka_Revue" title="Takarazuka Revue">Takarazuka Revue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bent-1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-bent-1-28">[28]</a></sup> These traits are most prominent in <a href="/wiki/Riyoko_Ikeda" title="Riyoko Ikeda">Riyoko Ikeda</a>'s works,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuricon_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-yuricon-29">[29]</a></sup> including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rose_of_Versailles" title="The Rose of Versailles">The Rose of Versailles</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Oniisama_e..." title="Oniisama e...">Oniisama e...</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Claudine...!" title="Claudine...!">Claudine...!</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alc-yuriko_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-alc-yuriko-30">[30]</a></sup> Some shōnen works of this period feature lesbian characters too, but these are mostly depicted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fanservice" title="Fanservice">fanservice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comic_relief" title="Comic relief">comic relief</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gen-1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-gen-1-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hito-nana1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="300" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1a/Hito-nana1.jpg/230px-Hito-nana1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1a/Hito-nana1.jpg 1.5x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hito-nana1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In general, manga from specialized <i>yuri</i> publications, such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuchibiru_Tameiki_Sakurairo" title="Kuchibiru Tameiki Sakurairo">Kuchibiru Tameiki Sakurairo</a></i>, display more explicit depictions of physical affection: from holding hands to kissing, and in some cases even sex scenes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some of these formulas began to weaken during the 1990s:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuri-esu_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-yuri-esu-10">[10]</a></sup> manga stories such as <i>Jukkai me no Jukkai</i> by Wakuni Akisato, published in 1992, began to move away from the tragic outcomes and stereotyped dynamics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symg_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-symg-32">[32]</a></sup> This stood side-by-side with <a href="/wiki/D%C5%8Djinshi" title="Dōjinshi">dōjinshi</a> works, which at the time were largely influenced by the immense popularity of <i><a href="/wiki/Sailor_Moon" title="Sailor Moon">Sailor Moon</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dou-yuri_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-dou-yuri-33">[33]</a></sup> the first mainstream manga and anime series featuring a "positive" portrayal of an openly <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Uranus" title="Sailor Uranus">lesbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Neptune" title="Sailor Neptune">couple</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about-2_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-about-2-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuricon_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-yuricon-29">[29]</a></sup> Furthermore, many of the people behind this show went on to make <i><a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Girl_Utena" title="Revolutionary Girl Utena">Revolutionary Girl Utena</a></i>, a shōjo anime series where the main storyline focuses on a <i>yuri</i> relationship, which is widely regarded today as a masterpiece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alc-afterellen_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-alc-afterellen-34">[34]</a></sup> Male-targeted works such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Devil_Lady" title="Devil Lady">Devilman Lady</a></i> anime series, based on a homonym <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seinen" title="Seinen">seinen</a> manga by Go Nagai, began to deal with lesbian themes in a more "mature manner" too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boredom_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-boredom-35">[35]</a></sup> The first magazines specifically targeted towards lesbians appeared around this period, containing sections featuring <i>yuri</i> manga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmilla_interview_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carmilla_interview-36">[36]</a></sup> These stories range from high school crush to lesbian life and love, featuring different degrees of sexual content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmilla_interview_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carmilla_interview-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-active-1_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-active-1-37">[37]</a></sup> It is at this point (the mid-1990s) that lesbian-themed works began to be acceptable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuricon_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-yuricon-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The later 1990s brought Oyuki Konno's <i><a href="/wiki/Maria-sama_ga_Miteru" title="Maria-sama ga Miteru">Maria-sama ga Miteru</a></i>, which by 2004 was a bestseller among <i>yuri</i> novels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl-mari1_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-gl-mari1-38">[38]</a></sup> This story revisits what was being written at the time of Nobuko Yoshiya:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esu-ex_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-esu-ex-39">[39]</a></sup> strong emotional bonds between females, mostly revolving around the school upperclassman-underclassman dynamic, like those portrayed in Class S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esu-ex_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-esu-ex-39">[39]</a></sup> Another prominent author of this period is Kaho Nakayama, active since the early 1990s, with works involving love stories among lesbians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl-mari1_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-gl-mari1-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around the early 2000s, the first magazines specifically dedicated to <i>yuri</i> manga were launched,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pedia-yuri_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-pedia-yuri-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pedia-yuri1_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-pedia-yuri1-12">[12]</a></sup> containing stories dealing with a wide range of themes: from intense emotional connections such as that depicted in <i><a href="/wiki/Voiceful" title="Voiceful">Voiceful</a></i>, to more explicit school-girl romances like those portrayed in <i><a href="/wiki/First_Love_Sisters" title="First Love Sisters">First Love Sisters</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven-new_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-seven-new-40">[40]</a></sup> and realistic tales about love between adult women such as those seen in <i>Rakuen no Jōken</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ichi-shima_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-ichi-shima-41">[41]</a></sup> Some of these subjects are seen in male-targeted works of this period as well,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bored-1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-bored-1-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ann-yuri-1_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-ann-yuri-1-43">[43]</a></sup> sometimes in combination with other themes, including <a href="/wiki/Mecha" title="Mecha">mecha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuri-ondvd_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-yuri-ondvd-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hime-s_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-hime-s-45">[45]</a></sup> Examples include series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kannazuki_no_Miko" title="Kannazuki no Miko">Kannazuki no Miko</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Blue_Drop" title="Blue Drop">Blue Drop</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Kashimashi:_Girl_Meets_Girl" title="Kashimashi: Girl Meets Girl">Kashimashi: Girl Meets Girl</a></i>. In addition, male-targeted stories tend to make extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Moe_(slang)" title="Moe (slang)">moe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bish%C5%8Djo" title="Bishōjo">bishōjo</a> characterizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ichi-yuri1_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-ichi-yuri1-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Publications">Publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yuri_(genre)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yuri_Shimai_Autumn_2004_cover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="300" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7f/Yuri_Shimai_Autumn_2004_cover.jpg/230px-Yuri_Shimai_Autumn_2004_cover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/7f/Yuri_Shimai_Autumn_2004_cover.jpg 1.5x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yuri_Shimai_Autumn_2004_cover.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cover of the autumn 2004 issue of <i>Yuri Shimai</i>, illustrated by Reine Hibiki, the illustrator for the <i>yuri</i> light novel series <i>Maria-sama ga Miteru</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sun Magazine published the <i>yuri</i> manga <a href="/wiki/Anthology" title="Anthology">anthology</a> magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Yuri_Shimai" title="Yuri Shimai">Yuri Shimai</a></i> between June 2003 and November 2004 in quarterly installments, ending with only five issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pedia-yuri_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-pedia-yuri-11">[11]</a></sup> After the magazine's discontinuation, <i><a href="/wiki/Comic_Yuri_Hime" title="Comic Yuri Hime">Comic Yuri Hime</a></i> was launched by <a href="/wiki/Ichijinsha" title="Ichijinsha">Ichijinsha</a> in July 2005 as a revival of the magazine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyzo_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-cyzo-5">[5]</a></sup> containing manga by many of the authors who had had work serialized in <i>Yuri Shimai</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pedia-yuri1_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-pedia-yuri1-12">[12]</a></sup> Like its predecessor, <i>Comic Yuri Hime</i> is also published quarterly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pedia-yuri1_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-pedia-yuri1-12">[12]</a></sup> A sister magazine to <i>Comic Yuri Hime</i> named <i><a href="/wiki/Comic_Yuri_Hime_S" title="Comic Yuri Hime S">Comic Yuri Hime S</a></i> was launched as a quarterly publication by Ichijinsha in June 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pedia-yuri2_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pedia-yuri2-46">[46]</a></sup> Unlike either <i>Yuri Shimai</i> or <i>Comic Yuri Hime</i>, <i>Comic Yuri Hime S</i> is targeted towards a male audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ichi-yuri1_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-ichi-yuri1-13">[13]</a></sup> Ichijinsha will start to publish <a href="/wiki/Light_novel" title="Light novel">light novel</a> adaptations from <i>Comic Yuri Hime</i> works and original <i>yuri</i> novels under their shōjo light novel line <i>Ichijinsha Bunko Iris</i>, scheduled to begin on July 19, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ichi-yuri2_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-ichi-yuri2-47">[47]</a></sup> Some Japanese lesbian lifestyle magazines contain manga sections, including the now-defunct magazines <i>Anise</i> (1996–97, 2001–03) and <i>Phryné</i> (1995).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmilla_interview_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carmilla_interview-36">[36]</a></sup> <i>Carmilla</i>, an erotic lesbian publication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmilla_interview_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Carmilla_interview-36">[36]</a></sup> released an anthology of lesbian manga called <i>Girl's Only</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Additionally, <i>Mist</i> (1996–99), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Josei" title="Josei">ladies' comic</a> manga magazine, contained sexually explicit lesbian-themed manga as part of a section dedicated to lesbian-interest topics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmilla_interview_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Carmilla_interview-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first company to release lesbian-themed manga in North America was <a href="/wiki/Yuricon" title="Yuricon">Yuricon</a>'s publishing arm ALC Publishing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuri-mags_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-yuri-mags-49">[49]</a></sup> Their works include <a href="/wiki/Rica_Takashima" title="Rica Takashima">Rica Takashima</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Rica_%27tte_Kanji!%3F" title="Rica 'tte Kanji!?">Rica 'tte Kanji!?</a></i>—which in 2006 was course material for Professor Kerridwen Luis' Anthropology 166B course at <a href="/wiki/Brandeis_University" title="Brandeis University">Brandeis University</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alc-con-x_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-alc-con-x-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anth-yuri_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-anth-yuri-51">[51]</a></sup>—and their annual <i>yuri</i> manga anthology <i>Yuri Monogatari</i>; both were first released in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuri-mags_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-yuri-mags-49">[49]</a></sup> The latter collects stories by American, European, and Japanese creators, including Akiko Morishima, Althea Keaton, Kristina Kolhi, Tomomi Nakasora, and Eriko Tadeno.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-press-yuri_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-press-yuri-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ota-1_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-ota-1-53">[53]</a></sup> These works range from fantasy stories to more realistic tales dealing with themes such as <a href="/wiki/Coming_out" title="Coming out">coming out</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ota-1_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-ota-1-53">[53]</a></sup> Besides ALC Publishing, the Los Angeles-based <a href="/wiki/Seven_Seas_Entertainment" title="Seven Seas Entertainment">Seven Seas Entertainment</a> has also incurred in the genre, with the English version of well known titles such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Kashimashi:_Girl_Meets_Girl" title="Kashimashi: Girl Meets Girl">Kashimashi: Girl Meets Girl</a></i> manga and the <i><a href="/wiki/Strawberry_Panic!" title="Strawberry Panic!">Strawberry Panic!</a></i> light novels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven-seas_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-seven-seas-20">[20]</a></sup> On October 24, 2006, Seven Seas announced the launch of their specialized <i>yuri</i> manga line, which includes works such as the <i>Strawberry Panic!</i> manga, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Uniform" title="The Last Uniform">The Last Uniform</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven-seas_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-seven-seas-20">[20]</a></sup> and <i>Comic Yuri Hime'</i>s compilations such as <i><a href="/wiki/Voiceful" title="Voiceful">Voiceful</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/First_Love_Sisters" title="First Love Sisters">First Love Sisters</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven-new_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-seven-new-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Yuri_series">Yuri series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yuri_(genre)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Yuri series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>These lists display stories according to the role <i>yuri</i> plays in them. The first list shows series in which <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_attraction" title="Interpersonal attraction">interpersonal attraction</a> between females and the incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Lesbian" title="Lesbian">lesbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Trope_(literature)" title="Trope (literature)">themes</a> play a central role in their genre or storylines. Such elements are labeled by publishers as <i>yuri</i>, and may include a lesbian character as the protagonist or a major character, or explorations of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexuality</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a> that deviate from the <a href="/wiki/Heteronormativity" title="Heteronormativity">hetero-normative</a>. The second list contains stories in which the same subjects are used mostly for <a href="/wiki/Comic_relief" title="Comic relief">comic relief</a>, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fanservice" title="Fanservice">fanservice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subtext" title="Subtext">subtext</a>, or for <a href="/wiki/Characterization" title="Characterization">character development</a> in a larger, sometimes unrelated context; these are generally recognized by the fandom as to contain prevalent elements of <i>yuri</i> (even if the series is not marketed as such).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yuri_as_a_central_element">Yuri as a central element</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yuri_(genre)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Yuri as a central element">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Iono-sama_Fanatics" title="Iono-sama Fanatics">Iono-sama Fanatics</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuri-iono_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-yuri-iono-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kannazuki_no_Miko" title="Kannazuki no Miko">Kannazuki no Miko</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yuri-ondvd_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-yuri-ondvd-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kashimashi:_Girl_Meets_Girl" title="Kashimashi: Girl Meets Girl">Kashimashi: Girl Meets Girl</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven-seas_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-seven-seas-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alc-afterellen_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-alc-afterellen-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kindred_Spirits_on_the_Roof" title="Kindred Spirits on the Roof">Kindred Spirits on the Roof</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
